mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject mw-editable page-East_Africa rootpage-East_Africa skin-vector action-view"> <div class="noprint" id="mw-page-base"></div>\n<div class="noprint" id="mw-head-base"></div>\n<div class="mw-body" id="content" role="main">\n<a id="top"></a>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">East Africa</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Eastern region of the African continent</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the entity known as (British) East Africa, see <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">East Africa Protectorate</a>. For the region of the African Union, see <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_the_African_Union#East" title="Regions of the African Union">Regions of the African Union &#167;&#160;East</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastern-Africa-map.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Eastern-Africa-map.PNG/220px-Eastern-Africa-map.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Eastern-Africa-map.PNG/330px-Eastern-Africa-map.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Eastern-Africa-map.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eastern-Africa-map.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eastern Africa (UN Statistics Division subregion)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LocationEasternAfrica.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/LocationEasternAfrica.png/240px-LocationEasternAfrica.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/LocationEasternAfrica.png 1.5x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LocationEasternAfrica.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00a000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Eastern Africa (<a href="/wiki/United_Nations_geoscheme" title="United Nations geoscheme">United Nations Statistics Division subregion</a>)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #177245; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ACE1AF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Rhodesia_and_Nyasaland" title="Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland">Central African Federation</a> (defunct)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00f000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a> (Counted as part of North Africa)</div></div></div></div>\n<p><b>East Africa</b> or <b>Eastern Africa</b> is the <a href="/wiki/East" title="East">eastern</a> <a href="/wiki/Region" title="Region">region</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a>, variably defined by <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_statistical_divisions" title="United Nations statistical divisions">United Nations Statistics Division scheme of geographic regions</a>, 20 territories make up Eastern Africa:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> &#8211; in Central East Africa, are members of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> (EAC). The first five are also included in the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region. Burundi and Rwanda are at times also considered to be part of <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> &#8211; collectively known as the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stock_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stock-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRIN_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-IRIN-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodd_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hodd-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joireman_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joireman-6">[6]</a></sup> The area is the easternmost projection of the African continent, and is sometimes considered a separate region from East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a> &#8211; small <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_in_the_Indian_Ocean" title="List of islands in the Indian Ocean">island nations</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union" title="R&#233;union">R&#233;union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mayotte" title="Mayotte">Mayotte</a> &#8211; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_overseas_departments_and_territories" title="French overseas departments and territories">French overseas territories</a> also in the Indian Ocean.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> &#8211; often considered part of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>, on the eastern side of the sub-continent. Madagascar has close cultural ties to <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and the islands of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> &#8211; often also included in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>, and formerly constituted the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Rhodesia_and_Nyasaland" title="Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland">Central African Federation</a> (also known historically as the <i>Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland</i>).</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> (newly independent from <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>) &#8211; collectively part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a>. Situated in the northeastern portion of the continent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EAPP_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-EAPP-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Sudans" title="The Sudans">the Sudans</a> are often included in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">Northern Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Also members of the <a href="/wiki/Common_Market_for_Eastern_and_Southern_Africa" title="Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa">Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa</a> (COMESA) free trade area.</li></ul>\n<p>Due to colonial territories of the British <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">East Africa Protectorate</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a>, the term <i>East Africa</i> is often (especially in the English language) used to specifically refer to the area now comprising the three countries of Kenya, Tanzania and Uganda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> However, this has never been the convention in many other languages, where the term generally had a wider, strictly geographic context and therefore typically included Djibouti, Eritrea, Ethiopia, and Somalia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Somaliland_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Somaliland-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" capitals and largest cities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_climate">Geography and climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography and href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aqua_rwanda_05jun04_250m.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1776" data-file-width="1772" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aqua_rwanda_05jun04_250m.jpg/200px-Aqua_rwanda_05jun04_250m.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aqua_rwanda_05jun04_250m.jpg/300px-Aqua_rwanda_05jun04_250m.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aqua_rwanda_05jun04_250m.jpg/400px-Aqua_rwanda_05jun04_250m.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aqua_rwanda_05jun04_250m.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of the region between <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> (on the right) and Lakes <a href="/wiki/Lake_Albert_(Africa)" title="Lake Albert (Africa)">Albert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Kivu" title="Lake Kivu">Kivu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Tanganyika</a> (from north to south) showing dense vegetation (bright green) and fires (red).</div></div></div>\n<p>Some parts of East Africa have been renowned for their concentrations of wild animals, such as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Five_Game" title="Big Five Game">big five</a>": the <a href="/wiki/African_elephant" title="African elephant">elephant</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_buffalo" title="African buffalo">buffalo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panthera_leo_melanochaita" title="Panthera leo melanochaita">lion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_rhinoceros" title="Black rhinoceros">black rhinoceros</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN,_Black_rhinoceros_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN,_Black_rhinoceros-21">[21]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopard</a>, though populations have been declining under increased stress in recent times, particularly those of the rhino and elephant.\n</p><p>The geography of East Africa is often stunning and scenic. Shaped by global plate tectonic forces that have created the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Rift" title="East African Rift">East African Rift</a>, East Africa is the site of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kenya" title="Mount Kenya">Mount Kenya</a>, the two tallest peaks in Africa. It also includes the world\'s second largest freshwater lake, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>, and the world\'s second deepest lake, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>.\n</p><p>The climate of East Africa is rather atypical of equatorial regions. Because of a combination of the region\'s generally high <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a> of the westerly <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> winds created by the <a href="/wiki/Rwenzori_Mountains" title="Rwenzori Mountains">Rwenzori Mountains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a>, East Africa is surprisingly cool and dry for its latitude. In fact, on the coast of Somalia, many years can go by without any rain whatsoever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Elsewhere the annual rainfall generally increases towards the south and with altitude, being around 400&#160;mm (16&#160;in) at <a href="/wiki/Mogadishu" title="Mogadishu">Mogadishu</a> and 1,200&#160;mm (47&#160;in) at <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> on the coast, whilst inland it increases from around 130&#160;mm (5&#160;in) at <a href="/wiki/Garoowe" title="Garoowe">Garoowe</a> to over 1,100&#160;mm (43&#160;in) at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moshi,_Kilimanjaro" title="Moshi, Kilimanjaro">Moshi</a> near Kilimanjaro. Unusually, most of the rain falls in <i>two</i> distinct <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">wet seasons</a>, one centred on April and the other in October or November. This is usually attributed to the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a> across the region in those months, but it may also be analogous to the autumn monsoon rains of parts of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> and the Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Northeast Region, Brazil">Nordeste</a>.\n</p><p>West of the Rwenzoris and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethiopian_highlands" title="Ethiopian highlands">Ethiopian highlands</a>, the rainfall pattern is more typically tropical, with rain throughout the year near the equator and a single wet season in most of the Ethiopian Highlands from June to September &#8211; contracting to July and August around <a href="/wiki/Asmara" title="Asmara">Asmara</a>. Annual rainfall here ranges from over 1,600&#160;mm (63&#160;in) on the western slopes to around 1,250&#160;mm (49&#160;in) at <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a> and 550&#160;mm (22&#160;in) at Asmara. In the high mountains rainfall can be over 2,500&#160;mm (98&#160;in).\n</p><p>Rainfall in East Africa is influenced by <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Ni&#241;o">El Ni&#241;o</a> events, which tend to increase rainfall except in the northern and western parts of the Ethiopian and Eritrean highlands, where they produce drought and poor <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> <a href="/wiki/Flooding_of_the_Nile" title="Flooding of the Nile">floods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Temperatures in East Africa, except on the hot and generally humid coastal belt, are moderate, with maxima of around 25&#160;&#176;C (77&#160;&#176;F) and minima of 15&#160;&#176;C (59&#160;&#176;F) at an altitude of 1,500 metres (4,921&#160;ft). At altitudes of above 2,500 metres (8,202&#160;ft), <a href="/wiki/Frost" title="Frost">frosts</a> are common during the dry season and maxima typically about 21&#160;&#176;C (70&#160;&#176;F) or less.\n</p><p>The unique geography and apparent suitability for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farming" title="Farming">farming</a> made East Africa a target for <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> <a href="/wiki/Exploration" title="Exploration">exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_natural_resources" title="Exploitation of natural resources">exploitation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialization</a> in the nineteenth century. Today, <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourism</a> is an important part of the economies of Kenya, Tanzania, Seychelles, and Uganda. The easternmost point of the continent, that is <a href="/wiki/Ras_Hafun" title="Ras Hafun">Ras Hafun</a> in Somalia, is of <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">historical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economy" title="Economy">economical</a> importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ncaaroth1975_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ncaaroth1975-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gssir_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gssir-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Recent African origin of modern humans</a></div>\n<p>According to the theory of the <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">recent African origin of modern humans</a>, the predominantly held belief among most archaeologists, East Africa is the area where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a> first appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agegmowhsh_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agegmowhsh-26">[26]</a></sup> There are differing theories on whether there was a single exodus or several; a multiple dispersal model involves the Southern Dispersal theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lahr_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-lahr-27">[27]</a></sup> A growing number of researchers suspect that <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> was instead the original home of the modern humans who first trekked out of the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21212332_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21212332-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The major competing hypothesis is the <a href="/wiki/Multiregional_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Multiregional origin of modern humans">multiregional origin of modern humans</a>, which envisions a wave of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> migrating earlier from Africa and interbreeding with local <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> populations in multiple regions of the globe. Most multiregionalists still view Africa as a major wellspring of human genetic diversity, but allow a much greater role for hybridization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JurmainKilgore2008_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-JurmainKilgore2008-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some of the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominin" title="Hominin">hominin</a> skeletal remains have been found in the wider region, including fossils discovered in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Awash_Valley" title="Awash Valley">Awash Valley</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, as well as in the <a href="/wiki/Koobi_Fora" title="Koobi Fora">Koobi Fora</a> in Kenya and <a href="/wiki/Olduvai_Gorge" title="Olduvai Gorge">Olduvai Gorge</a> in Tanzania.\n</p><p>The southern part of East Africa was occupied until recent times by <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a>, whereas in the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a> the <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkey</a> and such crop plants as <a href="/wiki/Teff" title="Teff">teff</a> allowed the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> around 7,000 B.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Lowland barriers and diseases carried by the <a href="/wiki/Tsetse_fly" title="Tsetse fly">tsetse fly</a>, however, prevented the donkey and agriculture from spreading southwards. Only in quite recent times has agriculture spread to the more humid regions south of the equator, through the spread of <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a> and crops such as <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a>. Language distributions suggest that this most likely occurred from Sudan into the African Great Lakes region, since the <a href="/wiki/Nilotic_languages" title="Nilotic languages">Nilotic languages</a> spoken by these pre-Bantu farmers have their closest relatives in the middle Nile basin.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_history">Ancient history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> coast of <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> are considered the most likely location of the land known to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptians</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Punt</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The old kingdom\'s first mention dates to the 25th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The ancient Puntites were a nation of people that had close relations with <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaonic Egypt</a> during the times of <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a> <a href="/wiki/Sahure" title="Sahure">Sahure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queen_regnant" title="Queen regnant">Queen</a> <a href="/wiki/Hatshepsut" title="Hatshepsut">Hatshepsut</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Kingdom of Aksum</a> was a trading empire centered in Eritrea and northern Ethiopia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> It existed from approximately 100&#8211;940 AD, growing from the proto-Aksumite <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> period c. 4th century BC to achieve prominence by the 1st century AD. The kingdom is mentioned in the <i><a href="/wiki/Periplus_of_the_Erythraean_Sea" title="Periplus of the Erythraean Sea">Periplus of the Erythraean Sea</a></i> as an important market place for <a href="/wiki/Ivory_trade" title="Ivory trade">ivory</a>, which was exported throughout the ancient world. Aksum was at the time ruled by <a href="/wiki/Zoskales" title="Zoskales">Zoskales</a>, who also governed the port of <a href="/wiki/Adulis" title="Adulis">Adulis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Periplusme_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Periplusme-35">[35]</a></sup> The Aksumite rulers facilitated trade by minting their own <a href="/wiki/Aksumite_currency" title="Aksumite currency">Aksumite currency</a>. The state also established its <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> over the declining <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Kush</a> and regularly entered the politics of the kingdoms on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>, eventually extending its rule over the region with the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Himyarite_Kingdom" title="Himyarite Kingdom">Himyarite Kingdom</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bantu_expansion">Bantu expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bantu expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a></div>\n<p>Between 2500&#8211;3000 years ago, <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a>-speaking peoples began a millennia-long series of migrations eastward from their homeland around southern Cameroon. This <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a> introduced agriculture into much of the African Great Lakes region. During the following fifteen centuries, the Bantu slowly intensified farming and grazing over all suitable regions of East Africa, in the process making contact with <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian</a>- and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>-speaking settlers on southern coastal areas. The latter also spread <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> to the coastal belt, but most Bantu remained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_Traditional_Religion" title="African Traditional Religion">African Traditional Religion</a> adherents.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:East_and_southern_africa_early_iron_age.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="784" data-file-width="677" decoding="async" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/East_and_southern_africa_early_iron_age.png/280px-East_and_southern_africa_early_iron_age.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/East_and_southern_africa_early_iron_age.png/420px-East_and_southern_africa_early_iron_age.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/East_and_southern_africa_early_iron_age.png/560px-East_and_southern_africa_early_iron_age.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:East_and_southern_africa_early_iron_age.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early Iron Age findings in East and Southern Africa</div></div></div>\n<p>Over a period of many centuries, most hunting-foraging peoples were displaced and absorbed by incoming Bantu communities, as well as by later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nilotic" title="Nilotic">Nilotic</a> communities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a> was a long series of physical migrations, a diffusion of language and knowledge out into and in from neighboring populations, and a creation of new societal groups involving inter-marriage among communities and small groups moving to communities and small groups moving to new areas.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>After their movements from their original homeland in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, Bantus also encountered in central east Africa peoples of <a href="/wiki/Cushitic_languages" title="Cushitic languages">Cushitic</a> origin. As cattle terminology in use amongst the few modern Bantu <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralist</a> groups suggests, the Bantu migrants would acquire <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> from their new Cushitic neighbors. Linguistic evidence also indicates that Bantus most likely borrowed the custom of milking cattle directly from Cushitic peoples in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the coastal section of the African Great Lakes region, another mixed Bantu community developed through contact with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> traders, leading to the development of the mixed Arab, Persian and African <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili City States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili culture</a> that emerged from these exchanges evinces many Arab and Islamic influences not seen in traditional Bantu culture, as do the many <a href="/wiki/Afro-Arab" title="Afro-Arab">Afro-Arab</a> members of the Bantu <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili people</a>. With its original speech community centered on the coastal parts of <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>&#8212;a seaboard referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a>&#8212;the Bantu <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili language</a> contains many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loan-words" title="Loan-words">loan-words</a> as a consequence of these interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest Bantu inhabitants of the east coast of Kenya and Tanzania encountered by these later Arab and Persian settlers have been variously identified with the trading settlements of <a href="/wiki/Rhapta" title="Rhapta">Rhapta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azania" title="Azania">Azania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menouthias" title="Menouthias">Menouthias</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> referenced in early Greek and Chinese writings from AD 50 to AD 500,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> ultimately giving rise to the name for <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> These early writings perhaps document the first wave of Bantu settlers to reach central east Africa during their migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between the 14th and 15th centuries, large African Great Lakes kingdoms and states emerged, such as the <a href="/wiki/Buganda" title="Buganda">Buganda</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roland_Oliver_1800._pp._24-25_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roland_Oliver_1800._pp._24-25-51">[51]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karagwe" title="Karagwe">Karagwe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roland_Oliver_1800._pp._24-25_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roland_Oliver_1800._pp._24-25-51">[51]</a></sup> kingdoms of Uganda and Tanzania.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_and_Portuguese_eras">Arab and Portuguese eras</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Arab and Portuguese eras">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_East_Africa" title="Portuguese East Africa">Portuguese East Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Oman" title="History of Oman">History of Oman</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portuguese</a> were the first Europeans to explore the region of current-day <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> by sea. <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> visited <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> in 1498. Da Gama\'s voyage was successful in reaching <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, which permitted the Portuguese to trade with the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a> directly by sea. This in turn challenged the older trading networks of mixed land and sea routes, such as the <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> routes which utilized the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camel_train" title="Camel train">camel caravans</a> to reach the eastern Mediterranean.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a> had gained control over much of the trade routes between Europe and Asia. After traditional land routes to India had been closed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turk" title="Ottoman Turk">Ottoman Turks</a>, Portugal hoped to use the sea route pioneered by da Gama to break the once Venetian trading monopoly. Portuguese rule in the African Great Lakes region focused mainly on a coastal strip centered around Mombasa. The Portuguese presence in the area officially began after 1505, when flagships under the command of <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Almeida" title="Francisco de Almeida">Don Francisco de Almeida</a> conquered <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Kisiwani" title="Kilwa Kisiwani">Kilwa</a>, an island located in what is now southern <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>.\n</p><p>In March 1505, having received from <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_of_Portugal" title="Manuel I of Portugal">Manuel I of Portugal</a> the appointment of viceroy of the newly conquered <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_India" title="Portuguese India">territory in India</a>, he set sail from <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> in command of a large and powerful fleet, and arrived in July at Quiloa (<a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Kisiwani" title="Kilwa Kisiwani">Kilwa</a>), which yielded to him almost without a struggle. A much more vigorous resistance was offered by the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> of Mombasa. However, the town was taken and destroyed, and its large treasures went to strengthen the resources of Almeida. Attacks followed on Hoja (now known as Ungwana, located at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Tana_River_(Kenya)" title="Tana River (Kenya)">Tana River</a>), Barawa, Angoche, <a href="/wiki/Pate_Island" title="Pate Island">Pate</a> and other coastal towns until the western <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> was a safe haven for Portuguese commercial interests. At other places on his way, such as the island of Angediva, near <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kannur" title="Kannur">Cannanore</a>, the Portuguese built forts, and adopted measures to secure the Portuguese supremacy.\n</p><p>Portugal\'s main goal on the Swahili coast was to take control of the spice trade from the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>. At this stage, the Portuguese presence in East Africa served the purposes of controlling trade within the Indian Ocean and securing the sea routes linking Europe to Asia. Portuguese naval vessels were very disruptive to the commerce of Portugal\'s enemies within the western Indian Ocean and were able to demand high tariffs on items transported through the sea due to their strategic control of ports and shipping lanes. The construction of <a href="/wiki/Fort_Jesus" title="Fort Jesus">Fort Jesus</a> in Mombasa in 1593 was meant to solidify Portuguese hegemony in the region, but their influence was clipped by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Omani</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> incursions into the Great Lakes region during the 17th century.\n</p><p>The Omani Arabs posed the most direct challenge to Portuguese influence in the African Great Lakes region. They besieged Portuguese fortresses, openly attacked naval vessels and expelled the Portuguese from the Kenyan and Tanzanian coasts by 1730. By this time, the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> had already lost its interest on the spice trade sea route due to the decreasing profitability of that business. The Arabs reclaimed much of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade" title="Indian Ocean trade">Indian Ocean trade</a>, forcing the Portuguese to retreat south where they remained in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_East_Africa" title="Portuguese East Africa">Portuguese East Africa</a> (Mozambique) as sole rulers until the 1975 independence of Mozambique.\n</p><p>Omani Arab <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a> of the Kenyan and Tanzanian coasts brought the once independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City-states" title="City-states">city-states</a> under closer foreign scrutiny and domination than was experienced during the Portuguese period. Like their predecessors, the Omani Arabs were primarily able only to control the coastal areas, not the interior. However, the creation of clove <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plantations" title="Plantations">plantations</a>, intensification of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a> and relocation of the Omani capital to <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> in 1839 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seyyid_Said" title="Seyyid Said">Seyyid Said</a> had the effect of consolidating the Omani power in the region.\n</p><p>Arab governance of all the major ports along the Swahili coast continued until British interests aimed particularly at ending the slave trade and creation of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wage-labour" title="Wage-labour">wage-labour</a> system began to put pressure on Omani rule. By the late nineteenth century, the slave trade on the open seas had been completely outlawed by the British and the Omani Arabs had little ability to resist the British navy\'s ability to enforce the directive. The Omani presence continued in Zanzibar and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pemba,_Tanzania" title="Pemba, Tanzania">Pemba</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Revolution" title="Zanzibar Revolution">Zanzibar Revolution</a> in 1964. However, the official Omani Arab presence in Kenya was checked by German and British seizure of key ports and creation of crucial trade alliances with influential local leaders in the 1880s.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Period_of_European_imperialism">Period of European imperialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Period of European imperialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Britisheastafrica_1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="823" data-file-width="684" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Britisheastafrica_1.png/220px-Britisheastafrica_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Britisheastafrica_1.png/330px-Britisheastafrica_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Britisheastafrica_1.png/440px-Britisheastafrica_1.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Britisheastafrica_1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of British East Africa in 1911</div></div></div>\n<p>Between the 19th and 20th century, East Africa became a theatre of competition between the major imperialistic European nations of the time. The three main colors of the African country were beige, red, and blue. The red stood for the English, blue stood for the French, and the beige stood for Germany during the period of colonialism. During the period of the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>, almost every country in the larger region to varying degrees became part of a European <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">colonial</a> empire.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> had first established a strong presence in southern <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and the Indian Ocean since the 15th century, while during this period their possessions increasingly grew including parts from the present northern Mozambique country, up to <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> in present-day Kenya. At <a href="/wiki/Lake_Malawi" title="Lake Malawi">Lake Malawi</a>, they finally met the recently created British <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">Protectorate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nyasaland" title="Nyasaland">Nyasaland</a> (nowadays <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>), which surrounded the homonymous lake on three sides, leaving the Portuguese the control of lake\'s eastern coast. The <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> set foot in the region\'s most exploitable and promising lands acquiring what is today <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>. The Protectorate of <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> and the Colony of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> were located in a rich farmland area mostly appropriate for the cultivation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cash_crops" title="Cash crops">cash crops</a> like <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>, as well as for animal husbandry with products produced from cattle and goats, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_goat" title="Domestic goat">goat meat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> and <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>. Moreover, this area had the potential for a significant residential expansion, being suitable for the relocation of a large number of British nationals to the region. Prevailing climatic conditions and the regions\' <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">geomorphology</a> allowed the establishment of flourishing European style settlements like <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vila_Pery" title="Vila Pery">Vila Pery</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vila_Junqueiro" title="Vila Junqueiro">Vila Junqueiro</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porto_Am%C3%A9lia" title="Porto Am&#233;lia">Porto Am&#233;lia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maputo" title="Maputo">Louren&#231;o Marques</a> and <a href="/wiki/Entebbe" title="Entebbe">Entebbe</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> settled the largest island of the Indian Ocean (and the fourth-largest globally), <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, along with a group of smaller islands nearby, namely <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union" title="R&#233;union">R&#233;union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a>. Madagascar <a href="/wiki/French_Madagascar" title="French Madagascar">became part</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_colonial_empires" title="French colonial empires">French colonial empire</a> following <a href="/wiki/Second_Madagascar_expedition" title="Second Madagascar expedition">two military campaigns</a> against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Madagascar" title="Kingdom of Madagascar">Kingdom of Madagascar</a>, which it initiated after persuading Britain to relinquish its interests in the island in exchange for control of <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika" title="Tanganyika">Tanganyika</a>, an important island hub of the <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">spices</a> trade. The British also held a number of island colonies in the region, including the extended <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> of <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a> and the rich farming island of <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, previously under the French <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> gained control of a large area named <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a>, comprising present-day <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a> and the mainland part of <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> named Tanganyika. In 1922, the British gained a <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">League of Nations mandate</a> over Tanganyika which it administered until Independence was granted to Tanganyika in 1961. Following the Zanzibar Revolution of 1965, the independent state of Tanganyika formed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Republic_of_Tanzania" title="United Republic of Tanzania">United Republic of Tanzania</a> by creating a union between the mainland, and the island chain of Zanzibar. Zanzibar is now a semi-autonomous state in a union with the mainland which is collectively and commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>. German East Africa, though very extensive, was not of such strategic importance as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_monarchy" title="British monarchy">British Crown\'s</a> colonies to the north: the inhabitation of these lands was difficult and thus limited, mainly due to climatic conditions and the local geomorphology. Italy gained control of various parts of <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> in the 1880s. The southern three-fourths of Somalia became an Italian <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a> (<a href="/wiki/Italian_Somaliland" title="Italian Somaliland">Italian Somaliland</a>).\n</p><p>Meanwhile, in 1884, a narrow coastal strip of northern Somalia came under British control (<a href="/wiki/British_Somaliland" title="British Somaliland">British Somaliland</a>). This northern protectorate was just opposite the British colony of <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>. With these territories secured, Britain was able to serve as gatekeeper of the <a href="/wiki/Sea_lane" title="Sea lane">sea lane</a> leading to <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a>. In 1890, beginning with the purchase of the small port town of (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asseb" title="Asseb">Asseb</a>) from a local sultan in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, the Italians colonized all of Eritrea.\n</p><p>In 1895, from bases in Somalia and Eritrea, the Italians launched the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Italo%E2%80%93Ethiopian_War" title="First Italo&#8211;Ethiopian War">First Italo&#8211;Ethiopian War</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Orthodox</a> Empire of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>. By 1896, the war had become a total disaster for the Italians and Ethiopia was able to retain its independence. Ethiopia remained independent until 1936 when, after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War">Second Italo-Abyssinian War</a>, it became part of <a href="/wiki/Italian_East_Africa" title="Italian East Africa">Italian East Africa</a>. The Italian occupation of Ethiopia ended in 1941 during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_II)" title="East African Campaign (World War II)">East African Campaign</a>.The French also staked out an East African outpost on the route to <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a>. Starting in the 1850s, the small protectorate of <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a> became <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a> in 1897.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic languages</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niger-Congo_languages" title="Niger-Congo languages">Niger-Congo languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan languages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic languages</a> predominate, including languages of the family\'s <a href="/wiki/Cushitic_languages" title="Cushitic languages">Cushitic</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Beja_language" title="Beja language">Beja</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oromo_language" title="Oromo language">Oromo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a>), <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Amharic" title="Amharic">Amharic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tigrinya_language" title="Tigrinya language">Tigrinya</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Omotic_languages" title="Omotic languages">Omotic</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Wolaytta_language" title="Wolaytta language">Wolaytta</a>) branches.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niger-Congo_languages" title="Niger-Congo languages">Niger-Congo languages</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a> branch are most widely spoken. Among these languages are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gikuyu_language" title="Gikuyu language">Kikuyu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kinyarwanda" title="Kinyarwanda">Kinyarwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirundi" title="Kirundi">Kirundi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runyakitara" title="Runyakitara">Runyakitara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luganda" title="Luganda">Luganda</a>. <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>, with at least 80 million speakers as a first or second language, is an important trade language in the Great Lakes area. It has official status in Tanzania, Kenya and Uganda. \n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Luo_languages" title="Luo languages">Luo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nandi%E2%80%93Markweta_languages" title="Nandi&#8211;Markweta languages">Kalenjin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maasai_language" title="Maasai language">Maasai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuer_language" title="Nuer language">Nuer</a>, are spoken in lesser numbers, primarily in the African Great Lakes and Nile Valley. \n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, remain important in higher institutions in some parts of the larger region.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Africa" title="Demographics of Africa">Demographics of Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_countries_by_population" title="List of African countries by population">List of African countries by population</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_African_ethnic_groups" title="List of African ethnic groups">List of African ethnic groups</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nilotic_expansion" title="Nilotic expansion">Nilotic expansion</a></div>\n<p>Eastern Africa had an estimated population of 260 million in 2000. This was projected to reach 890 million by 2050, with an average growth rate of 2.5% per annum. The 2000 population is expected to quintuple over the course of the 21st century, to 1.6 billion as of 2100 (UN estimates as of 2017).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2017_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN2017-52">[52]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Ethiopia" title="Demographics of Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, there is an estimated population of 102 million as of 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflicts">Conflicts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Conflicts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Until recently, several East African countries were riven with political coups, ethnic violence and oppressive dictators. Since the end of colonialism, the region has endured the following conflicts:\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Northern East Africa (Horn of Africa)</dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Civil_War" title="Ethiopian Civil War">Ethiopian Civil War</a> 1974&#8211;1991</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Eritrean_War_of_Independence" title="Eritrean War of Independence">Eritrean War of Independence</a> 1961&#8211;1991</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eritrean-Ethiopian_War" title="Eritrean-Ethiopian War">Eritrean-Ethiopian War</a> 1998&#8211;2000</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ogaden_War" title="Ogaden War">Ogaden War</a> 1977&#8211;1978</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War" title="Somali Civil War">Somali Civil War</a> 1991&#8211;2009</li></ul>\n<dl><dt>South Sudan</dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Second_Sudanese_Civil_War" title="Second Sudanese Civil War">Second Sudanese Civil War</a> 1983&#8211;2005</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_violence_in_South_Sudan_(2011%E2%80%93present)" title="Ethnic violence in South Sudan (2011&#8211;present)">Internal Political-ethnic Conflict</a> 2011-ongoing</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/South_Sudanese_Civil_War" title="South Sudanese Civil War">South Sudanese Civil War</a> 2013&#8211;2015</li></ul>\n<dl><dt>Southern East Africa (Southeast Africa)</dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burundi_Civil_War" title="Burundi Civil War">Burundi Civil War</a> 1993&#8211;2005 and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burundi_Genocide" title="Burundi Genocide">Genocide of Hutus in 1972 and genocide of Tutsis in 1993</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uganda-Tanzania_War" title="Uganda-Tanzania War">Uganda-Tanzania War</a> 1978&#8211;1979</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ugandan_Bush_War" title="Ugandan Bush War">Ugandan Bush War</a> 1981&#8211;1986</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Resistance_Army" title="Lord\'s Resistance Army">Lord\'s Resistance Army</a> insurgency in <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> ongoing</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Rwandan_Civil_War" title="Rwandan Civil War">Rwandan Civil War</a> 1990&#8211;1993 and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rwandan_Genocide" title="Rwandan Genocide">Rwandan Genocide of Tutsis</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Revolution" title="Zanzibar Revolution">Zanzibar Revolution</a> 1964</li></ul>\n<dl><dt>Outside Southeast Africa with Southeast African participation</dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/First_Congo_War" title="First Congo War">First Congo War</a> 1996&#8211;1997 and <a href="/wiki/Second_Congo_War" title="Second Congo War">Second Congo War</a> 1998&#8211;2003</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kivu_Conflict" title="Kivu Conflict">Kivu Conflict</a> (Laurent Nkunda Rebellion)</li></ul>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> has enjoyed relatively stable governance. However, politics have been turbulent at times, including the <a href="/wiki/1982_Kenyan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1982 Kenyan coup d\'&#233;tat attempt">attempted coup d&#8217;&#233;tat in 1982</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clashes_in_Kenya_(2007-present)" title="Clashes in Kenya (2007-present)">2007 election riots</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> has known stable government since independence although there are significant political and religious tensions resulting from the political union between Tanganyika and Zanzibar in 1964. Zanzibar is now a semi-autonomous state in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Republic_of_Tanzania" title="United Republic of Tanzania">United Republic of Tanzania</a>.\n</p><p>Tanzania and <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> fought the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uganda-Tanzania_War" title="Uganda-Tanzania War">Uganda-Tanzania War</a> in 1978&#8211;1979, which led to the removal of Uganda\'s despotic leader <a href="/wiki/Idi_Amin" title="Idi Amin">Idi Amin</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a> have each faced instability and ethnic conflict since independence, most notably the 1994 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rwandan_Genocide" title="Rwandan Genocide">Rwandan Genocide</a> and the 1993 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burundi_Genocide" title="Burundi Genocide">Burundi Genocide</a> and subsequent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burundi_Civil_War" title="Burundi Civil War">Burundi Civil War</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> continue to be involved in related conflicts outside the region.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Puntland" title="Puntland">Puntland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a> regions of Somalia, have also seen relative stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> peacefully seceded from <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> in 2011, six and a half years after a <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Peace_Agreement" title="Comprehensive Peace Agreement">peace agreement</a> ended the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sudanese_Civil_War" title="Second Sudanese Civil War">Second Sudanese Civil War</a>. South Sudanese independence was nearly derailed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Kordofan_conflict" title="South Kordofan conflict">South Kordofan conflict</a>, particularly a dispute over the status of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abyei_Area" title="Abyei Area">Abyei Area</a>, and both Abyei and South Kordofan\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuba_Hills" title="Nuba Hills">Nuba Hills</a> remain a source of tension between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juba,_South_Sudan" title="Juba, South Sudan">Juba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Countries.2C_capitals_and_largest_cities"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Countries,_capitals_and_largest_cities">Countries, capitals and largest cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Countries, capitals and largest cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_East_Africa" title="List of cities in East Africa">List of cities in East Africa</a></div>\n<p>According to the CIA, as of 2017, the countries in the eastern Africa region have a total population of around 537.9 million inhabitants.<sup
